<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="journal_id" content="41467"/>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="The genome evolution and low-phosphorus adaptation in white lupin"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Nature Communications 2020 11:1"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2020-02-26"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2020 The Author(s)"/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="White lupin (Lupinus albus) is a legume crop that develops cluster roots and has high phosphorus&#160;(P)-use efficiency (PUE) in low-P soils. Here, we assemble the genome of white lupin and find that it has evolved from a whole-genome triplication (WGT) event. We then decipher its diploid ancestral genome and reconstruct the three sub-genomes. Based on the results, we further reveal the sub-genome dominance and the genic expression of the different sub-genomes varying in relation to their transposable element (TE) density. The PUE genes in white lupin have been expanded through WGT as well as tandem and dispersed duplications. Furthermore, we characterize four main pathways for high PUE, which include carbon fixation, cluster root formation, soil-P remobilization, and cellular-P reuse. Among these, auxin modulation may be important for cluster root formation through involvement of potential genes LaABCG36s and LaABCG37s. These findings provide insights into the genome evolution and low-P adaptation of white lupin. White lupin is a legume crop that develops cluster roots for efficient phosphate acquisition. Here, the authors assemble its triplicated genome, reveal gene expression difference among sub-genomes, and characterize possible pathways related to high phosphorus-use efficiency."/>

of Agricultural Sciences, Key Laboratory of Biology and Genetic Improvement of Horticultural Crops of the Ministry of Agriculture, Sino-Dutch Joint Laboratory of Horticultural Genomics, Beijing, href="#Aff2">2</a><span itemprop="affiliation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Organization" class="u-visually-hidden"><meta itemprop="name" content="Sino-Dutch Joint Laboratory of itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Person"><span itemprop="name"><a data-test="author-name" data-track="click" data-track-action="open author" data-track-label="link"             <a class="js-orcid" itemprop="url" href="http://orcid.org/0000-0003-0941-8326"><span class="u-visually-hidden">ORCID: </span>orcid.org/0000-0003-0941-8326</a></span><sup class="u-js-hide"><a href="#Aff1">1</a><span itemprop="affiliation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Organization" class="u-visually-hidden"><meta itemprop="name" content="Fujian Agriculture and Forestry University" /><meta itemprop="address" content="0000 0004 1760 2876, grid.256111.0, Center for Plant Water-use and Nutrition Regulation and College of Life Sciences, Joint International Research Laboratory of Water and Nutrient in Crop, Fujian Agriculture and Forestry University, Jinshan, Fuzhou, 350002, China" /></span></sup> &amp; </li><li class="c-author-list__item" itemprop="author" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Person"><span itemprop="name"><a data-test="author-name" data-track="click" data-track-action="open author" data-track-label="link" href="#auth-Feng-Cheng" data-author-popup="auth-Feng-Cheng" data-corresp-id="c4">Feng Cheng<svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-email"></use></svg></a></span><span class="u-js-hide"> 
            <a class="js-orcid" itemprop="url" href="http://orcid.org/0000-0003-2982-9675"><span class="u-visually-hidden">ORCID: </span>orcid.org/0000-0003-2982-9675</a></span><sup class="u-js-hide"><a href="#Aff2">2</a><span itemprop="affiliation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Organization" class="u-visually-hidden"><meta itemprop="name" content="Sino-Dutch Joint Laboratory of Horticultural Genomics" /><meta itemprop="address" content="Institute of Vegetables and Flowers, Chinese Academy of Agricultural Sciences, Key Laboratory of Biology and Genetic Improvement of Horticultural Crops of the Ministry of Agriculture, Sino-Dutch Joint Laboratory of Horticultural Genomics, Beijing, China" /></span></sup> </li></ul>

            <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><p>White lupin (<i>Lupinus albus</i>) is a legume crop that develops cluster roots and has high phosphorus (P)-use efficiency (PUE) in low-P soils. Here, we assemble the genome of white lupin and find that it has evolved from a whole-genome triplication (WGT) event. We then decipher its diploid ancestral genome and reconstruct the three sub-genomes. Based on the results, we further reveal the sub-genome dominance and the genic expression of the different sub-genomes varying in relation to their transposable element (TE) density. The PUE genes in white lupin have been expanded through WGT as well as tandem and dispersed duplications. Furthermore, we characterize four main pathways for high PUE, which include carbon fixation, cluster root formation, soil-P remobilization, and cellular-P reuse. Among these, auxin modulation may be important for cluster root formation through involvement of potential genes <i>LaABCG36</i>s and <i>LaABCG37</i>s. These findings provide insights into the genome evolution and low-P adaptation of white lupin.</p></div></div></section>
                <noscript>
                    
                </noscript>

                    </div>
                
                
                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Introduction"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Introduction</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>Phosphorus (P) is required for plant growth. P is often present in the soil in unavailable forms, such as phytic acid, or calcium (Ca), iron (Fe), and aluminum (Al) phosphates<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Xu, W., Shi, W., Jia, L., Liang, J. &amp; Zhang, J. TFT6 and TFT7, two different members of tomato 14-3-3 gene family, play distinct roles in plant adaption to low phosphorus stress. Plant Cell Environ. 35, 1393–1406 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d56465e481">1</a></sup>, and these forms are difficult for plants to absorb. About 5.7 billion hectares of land worldwide contain too little P for crop growth, and P deficiency constrains agricultural productivity<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Uhde-Stone, C. in Legume Nitrogen Fixation in Soils with Low Phosphorus Availability 243–280 (Springer, 2017)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d56465e485">2</a></sup>. To cope with P-limiting environments, several processes have evolved in plants. These include morphological, physiological, biochemical, and molecular adaptations<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Vance, C. P., Uhde‐Stone, C. &amp; Allan, D. L. Phosphorus acquisition and use: critical adaptations by plants for securing a nonrenewable resource. N. Phytol. 157, 423–447 (2003)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d56465e489">3</a></sup>.</p><p>White lupin (<i>Lupinus albus</i>, 2<i>n</i> = 50) belonging to the family Fabaceae can mobilize soil phosphates by the formation of densely packed lateral roots. These are also called cluster roots or proteoid roots. White lupin can grow without the addition of P fertilizer or help from mycorrhizal fungi, and its ability to fix dinitrogen (N<sub>2</sub>) is less inhibited by P deficiency compared to other legumes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Uhde-Stone, C. in Legume Nitrogen Fixation in Soils with Low Phosphorus Availability 243–280 (Springer, 2017)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d56465e504">2</a></sup>. The morphological and physiological factors of low-P adaptation in white lupin have been well studied<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Cheng, L. et al. Interactions between light intensity and phosphorus nutrition affect the phosphate-mining capacity of white lupin (Lupinus albus L.). J. Exp. Bot. 65, 2995–3003 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d56465e508">4</a></sup>. However, the genome evolution and low-P adaptation in white lupin is unclear.</p><p>All plant species have experienced polyploidization events<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Salman-Minkov, A., Sabath, N. &amp; Mayrose, I. Whole-genome duplication as a key factor in crop domestication. Nat. Plants 2, 16115 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d56465e515">5</a></sup>, which are involved in plant speciation and evolution of new functions. Narrow-leaf lupin (<i>Lupinus angustifolius</i>) is a close relative of white lupin. <i>Lupinus angustifolius</i> experienced a hexaploidization event<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Hane, J. K. et al. A comprehensive draft genome sequence for lupin (Lupinus angustifolius), an emerging health food: insights into plant-microbe interactions and legume evolution. Plant Biotechnol. J. 15, 318–330 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d56465e525">6</a></sup> that was shared with white lupin. Based on genomic block (GB) systems<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Schranz, M. E., Lysak, M. A. &amp; Mitchell-Olds, T. The ABC’s of comparative genomics in the Brassicaceae: building blocks of crucifer genomes. Trends Plant Sci. 11, 535–542 (2006)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d56465e529">7</a></sup>, diploid ancestors of paleopolyploids have been found in <i>Brassica</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Cheng, F. et al. Deciphering the diploid ancestral genome of the mesohexaploid Brassica rapa. Plant Cell 25, 1541–1554 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d56465e536">8</a></sup> and in the Poaceae<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Murat, F. et al. Ancestral grass karyotype reconstruction unravels new mechanisms of genome shuffling as a source of plant evolution. Genome Res. 20, 1545–1557 (2010)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d56465e540">9</a></sup>. After allopolyploidization events, sub-genome dominance phenomena are important features shaping the evolution of polyploid genomes. Sub-genome dominance indicates that one sub-genome has a higher gene density than the others. In addition, there are more genes from this sub-genome expressed at higher levels than their paralogs from the other sub-genomes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Schnable, J. C., Springer, N. M. &amp; Freeling, M. Differentiation of the maize subgenomes by genome dominance and both ancient and ongoing gene loss. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 108, 4069–4074 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d56465e544">10</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Cheng, F. et al. Biased gene fractionation and dominant gene expression among the subgenomes of Brassica rapa. PLoS ONE 7, e36442 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d56465e547">11</a></sup>. Transposable elements (TEs) have been shown to play a role in dominant expression of paralogous genes between sub-genomes in <i>Brassica rapa</i> and maize<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Woodhouse, M. R. et al. Origin, inheritance, and gene regulatory consequences of genome dominance in polyploids. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 111, 5283–5288 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d56465e554">12</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Cheng, F. et al. Epigenetic regulation of subgenome dominance following whole genome triplication in Brassica rapa. N. Phytol. 211, 288–299 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d56465e557">13</a></sup>. However, they do not show low-P adaptation. Knowledge of the evolution of the paleo-genome and sub-genomes in white lupin will contribute to our understanding of its adaptations to low P levels.</p><p>In this study, we use the long-read sequencing of PacBio technology combined with high-throughput chromatin capture (Hi-C) datasets, as well as mRNA-sequencing (mRNA-seq), comparative and evolutionary genomic analysis, pharmacology assays, genetic transformation, physiology, and biochemistry analyses to characterize the reference genome of white lupin and investigate its chromosomal evolution and the molecular basis of its adaptation to low-P availability.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Results"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Results</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Pseudo-chromosome construction of the white lupin genome</h3><p>We assembled the genome of the white lupin cultivar Amiga with combined datasets from third-generation long-read SMRT sequencing (PacBio) and long-range, Hi-C sequencing. We confirmed that the white lupin plant used for sequencing had 25 pairs of chromosomes using in situ hybridization (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#MOESM1">1</a>). We then generated 60 Gb Illumina Solexa 150 bp paired-end reads data and estimated the genome size of white lupin as 584.51 Mb by 17 K-mer counting. We produced 84.29 Gb (~144×) PacBio reads data (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#MOESM1">1</a>) and assembled the data into contigs using the software Canu<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Koren, S. et al. Canu: scalable and accurate long-read assembly via adaptive k-mer weighting and repeat separation. Genome Res. 27, 722–736 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d56465e582">14</a></sup>, followed by sequence polish<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Utturkar, S. M., Klingeman, D. M., Hurt, R. A. Jr. &amp; Brown, S. D. A case study into microbial genome assembly gap sequences and finishing strategies. Front. Microbiol. 8, 1272 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d56465e586">15</a></sup> and filtering. We obtained 3171 contigs with a total size of 558.74 Mb. The contig N50 was 1.76 Mb; the largest contig was 9.48 Mb (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#Tab1">1</a>). A Hi-C library was constructed and generated ~100-fold coverage of Hi-C linkage data (100 bp paired-end reads). We then linked the contigs into scaffolds based on the Hi-C data<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="van Berkum, N. L. et al. Hi-C: a method to study the three-dimensional architecture of genomes. J. Vis. Exp. 6, e1869 (2010)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d56465e594">16</a></sup> with the help of a previously published linkage map<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Ksiazkiewicz, M. et al. A high-density consensus linkage map of white lupin highlights synteny with narrow-leafed lupin and provides markers tagging key agronomic traits. Sci. Rep. 7, 15335 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d56465e598">17</a></sup> (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#MOESM1">2</a>). Finally, we obtained 1580 scaffolds, and the scaffold N50 was 18.66 Mb (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#Tab1">1</a>). The 25 largest scaffolds comprised 1616 contigs, which accounted for 84.87% (474.20 Mb) of the assembled genome and corresponded to the 25 chromosomes of white lupin (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#MOESM1">2</a> and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#MOESM1">3</a>). We compared this assembled genome with a currently released white lupin genome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Hufnagel, B. et al. High-quality genome sequence of white lupin provides insight into soil exploration and seed quality. Nat. Commun. 11, 492 (2020)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d56465e615">18</a></sup>, and found that they have a good chromosomal synteny relationship, except for some small-scale segment inversions (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#MOESM1">4</a>).</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-1"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab1" data-test="table-caption">Table 1 The assembly statistics of white lupin genome.</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z/tables/1"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>We combined de novo prediction, homology search, and mRNA-seq assisted prediction to predict genes in the genome of white lupin (see Methods), and we obtained 48,719 genes (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#MOESM1">3</a> and Supplementary Tables <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#MOESM1">3</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#MOESM1">4</a>). BUSCO (Benchmarking Universal Single-Copy Ortholog) analysis with embryophyta datasets<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Seppey, M., Manni, M. &amp; Zdobnov, E. M. BUSCO: assessing genome assembly and annotation completeness. Methods Mol. Biol. 1962, 227–245 (2019)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d56465e735">19</a></sup> estimated that 95.20% of the conserved single-copy genes were predicted. TEs are a major component of the white lupin genome. We used the tools RepeatModeler, RepeatMasker, and RepeatProteinMask to predict TEs from the white lupin genome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Tarailo-Graovac, M. &amp; Chen, N. Using RepeatMasker to identify repetitive elements in genomic sequences. Curr. Protoc. Bioinform. 4, 10 (2009)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d56465e739">20</a></sup> (see Methods). The combined size of all TE sequences was 262.71 Mb and occupied 43.86% of the assembled genome (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#MOESM1">5</a> and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#MOESM1">3</a>). Among the sequences, long terminal repeats were the major component (37.81%).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">The diploid ancestral genome of paleohexaploid white lupin</h3><p><i>Lupinus albus</i> and <i>L. angustifolius</i> experienced a common whole-genome triplication (WGT) event. We compared the genome of white lupin to 15 other legume species with sequenced genomes (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#MOESM1">6</a>). <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i> was used as the outgroup. First, we determined syntenic gene pairs between pairs of the 16 legume genomes using SynOrths<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Cheng, F., Wu, J., Fang, L. &amp; Wang, X. Syntenic gene analysis between Brassica rapa and other Brassicaceae species. Front. Plant Sci. 3, 198 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d56465e769">21</a></sup>. From these syntenic gene datasets, we obtained 1664 homologous genes that were shared by all 16 genomes. We picked 78,926 synonymous sites from these homologous genes to build a phylogenetic tree (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#Fig1">1a</a>). The times of divergence between legume species were estimated by calculating <i>K</i><sub>s</sub> values (the rate of synonymous mutations per synonymous locus) between syntenic genes (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#Fig1">1b</a>). The results showed that <i>L. albus</i> has the closest relationship to <i>L. angustifolius</i> (<i>K</i><sub>s</sub> ~=0.12, divergent ~9.40 million years) (see Methods), followed by a group of combined species from Galegoid and Millettioid that contains <i>Cicer arietinum</i> and <i>Phaseolus vulgaris</i> (<i>L. albus</i> to <i>P. vulgaris</i>, <i>K</i><sub>s</sub> ~=0.51, divergent ~39.97 million years), then by the group of Dalbergioid that contains the two diploid ancestors of cultivated peanut <i>Arachis ipaensis</i> and <i>Arachis duranensis</i> (<i>L. albus</i> to <i>A. ipaensis</i>, <i>K</i><sub>s</sub> ~= 0.52, divergent ~40.75 million years) (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#Fig1">1</a>). Two relatively recent polyploidization events were found in these analyzed legume species: one is a WGT (<i>K</i><sub>s</sub> ~=0.28, ~21.94 million years) event shared by <i>L. albus</i> and <i>L. angustifolius</i> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#Fig1">1</a> and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#MOESM1">5</a>), and the other is a whole-genome duplication (WGD, <i>K</i><sub>s</sub> ~=0.11, ~8.62 million years) event shared by wild and domesticated soybeans <i>Glycine max</i> and <i>Glycine soja</i>.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Phylogenetic relationships of white lupin and its genome comparison to the other legume species."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 1: Phylogenetic relationships of white lupin and its genome comparison to the other legume species.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-020-14891-z/MediaObjects/41467_2020_14891_Fig1_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-020-14891-z/MediaObjects/41467_2020_14891_Fig1_HTML.png" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="438" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p><b>a</b> Phylogenetic tree of 16 legume species built on synonymous sites from syntenic homologous genes; <i>A. thaliana</i> was used as an outgroup species. The two red stars denote the whole-genome duplication event, while the three red stars denote the whole-genome triplication event. <b>b</b> Frequency distribution of <i>K</i><sub>s</sub> values between syntenic genes of compared genomes. <b>c</b> Dot plotting of syntenic genes between genomes of <i>L. albus</i> and <i>L. angustifolius</i>. <b>d</b> Dot plotting of syntenic genes between genomes of <i>L. albus</i> and <i>P</i>. <i>vulgaris</i>; the segments from the three sub-genomes in <i>L. albus</i> are plotted in red, green, and blue. The source data underlying Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#MOESM6">1b</a> are provided as a Source Data file.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>The genome of white lupin retained three copies of genomic fragments that show syntenic relationships with the diploid genome of <i>P. vulgaris</i>. Based on syntenic gene pairs between <i>L. albus</i> and <i>P. vulgaris</i>, we extracted 167 fragments longer than 200 kb in white lupin that showed synteny to the genome of <i>P. vulgaris</i> (see Methods). These syntenic fragments corresponded to 73.39% and 93.41% of <i>L. albus</i> and <i>P. vulgaris</i> genomes, respectively (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#Fig1">1d</a>). These white lupin-specific genomic regions are rich in TE sequences (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#MOESM1">3</a>). The repeat sequence-enriched regions may not have been assembled by short reads, or they may not exist in the <i>P. vulgaris</i> genome. Based on the syntenic relationships, we found three copies of <i>L. albus</i> genomic fragments syntenic to each genomic region in <i>P. vulgaris</i> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#Fig1">1d</a>).</p><p>The diploid ancestral genome of white lupin probably had nine chromosomes. Based on the chromosome syntenic relationships between <i>Lupinus</i> and the diploid genome of <i>P. vulgaris</i> from Millettioid, we defined a legume-specific GB system to aid comparative genome analysis of <i>L. albus</i>. The chromosome regions in <i>P. vulgaris</i> (used as a reference) that corresponded to breakpoints appearing in more than one sub-genome in <i>Lupinus</i> (both <i>L. albus</i> and <i>L. angustifolius</i>) were counted and considered as sequence associations that did not exist in the diploid ancestral genome of <i>Lupinus</i> (see Methods) (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#Fig1">1d</a> and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#MOESM1">5</a>). A total of 14 such breakpoints were found in the 11 chromosomes of <i>P. vulgaris</i>; these breakpoints split the <i>P. vulgaris</i> genome into 26 GBs (A–Z) (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#Fig2">2a</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#MOESM1">7</a>). We further mapped the 26 GBs onto the 25 chromosomes of white lupin (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#Fig2">2</a>) and screened the white lupin genome to find GB associations that appeared in more than one sub-genome. A total of 18 such GB associations were identified (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#MOESM1">8</a>). These GB associations may have existed in the diploid ancestral genome of <i>Lupinus</i>. We concluded that the <i>Lupinus</i> diploid ancestor had nine chromosomes and followed the GB orders shown in Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#Fig2">2c</a>. The same results were obtained when we used <i>M. truncatula</i> from Galegoid and <i>Arachis ipaensis</i> from Dalbergioid as the references to deduce the diploid ancestor of <i>Lupinus</i> (Supplementary Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#MOESM1">6</a>–<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#MOESM1">8</a>). The genome also shows a karyotype structure similar to previous studies<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Ren, L., Huang, W. &amp; Cannon, S. B. Reconstruction of ancestral genome reveals chromosome evolution history for selected legume species. N. Phytol. 223, 2090–2103 (2019)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d56465e1029">22</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Kreplak, J. et al. A reference genome for pea provides insight into legume genome evolution. Nat. Genet. 51, 1411–1422 (2019)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d56465e1032">23</a></sup>. With the diploid ancestor as the reference, we further reconstructed the ancestral status of the three sub-genomes in white lupin (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#MOESM1">9</a>). Additionally, we found that two genomic fragments in <i>P. vulgaris</i> show variations among legume genomes (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#MOESM1">9</a>). One fragment located in the middle of chromosome Pv04 and between GBs G and H has been lost in the genome of <i>M. truncatula</i> (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#MOESM1">6</a>), while another located at the head of chromosome Pv06 and before block K has been lost in the genome of <i>A. ipaensis</i> (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#MOESM1">8</a>), and both fragments have been lost in genomes of <i>L. albus</i> and <i>L. angustifolius</i> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#Fig1">1d</a> and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#MOESM1">5</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Genome evolution trajectory of white lupin."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 2: Genome evolution trajectory of white lupin.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-020-14891-z/MediaObjects/41467_2020_14891_Fig2_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-020-14891-z/MediaObjects/41467_2020_14891_Fig2_HTML.png" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="810" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p><b>a</b> Distribution of 26 genomic blocks (GBs) in the 11 chromosomes of <i>P. vulgaris</i>. <b>b</b> The re-arranged GBs in the eight chromosomes of <i>M. truncatula</i>. The numbers to the right of the GBs denote the order of fragmented GBs. <b>c</b> Distribution of 26 GBs in the deduced nine ancestral chromosomes of the diploid ancestor of white lupin. <b>d</b> Distribution of the triplicated 26 GBs in the 25 chromosomes of white lupin; the colors red, green, and blue indicate GBs from sub-genomes LF, MF1, and MF2, respectively. “′” denote inverted GBs.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Sub-genome dominance and biased TE distribution in <i>L. albus</i>
</h3><p>Sub-genome dominance has been observed in many allopolyploid genomes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Schnable, J. C., Springer, N. M. &amp; Freeling, M. Differentiation of the maize subgenomes by genome dominance and both ancient and ongoing gene loss. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 108, 4069–4074 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d56465e1109">10</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Cheng, F. et al. Biased gene fractionation and dominant gene expression among the subgenomes of Brassica rapa. PLoS ONE 7, e36442 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d56465e1112">11</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Sankoff, D. &amp; Zheng, C. Fractionation, rearrangement and subgenome dominance. Bioinformatics 28, i402–i408 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d56465e1115">24</a></sup>, and it also exists in white lupin. We compared gene density among the three sub-genomes of white lupin in units of ancestral chromosomes. One chromosome copy always had significantly more genes (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#MOESM1">10</a>) than the other two copies. One sub-genome had a higher gene density than the other two. This was true except for the head of GB K and the tails of GBs A, S, and H, in which large fragment deletions occurred (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#Fig3">3a</a>). Copies of nine ancestral chromosomes that had the highest gene density were grouped and named as sub-genome LF (the least fractionated sub-genome), while the copies of nine chromosomes with moderately fractionated genes were grouped as sub-genome MF1 (more fractionated sub-genome 1). The remaining copy was grouped as sub-genome MF2 (more fractionated sub-genome 2). Among the <i>L. albus</i> genes that had syntenic orthologs in <i>P. vulgaris</i>, 7598, 6227, and 5567 genes were located in sub-genomes LF, MF1, and MF2, respectively. Sub-genome LF retained significantly more genes after WGT than sub-genomes MF1 and MF2 (<i>P</i> value = 4.50 × 10<sup>−36</sup>, <i>χ</i><sup>2</sup> test) (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#MOESM1">10</a>). Dominant expression was observed between paralogous genes from the three sub-genomes of white lupin. Based on the combined information of syntenic genes and sub-genome partitions, we determined 2673, 2418, and 1948 pairwise paralogs between sub-genomes LF and MF1, LF and MF2, and MF1 and MF2, respectively. Using expression data of the white lupin leaf as an example, among these 2673 paralogous genes between LF and MF1, the sub-genome LF (637) had significantly more genes expressed at higher levels than MF1 (533) (two-fold change) (<i>P</i> value = 2.59 × 10<sup>−3</sup>, binomial test) (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#Fig3">3b</a>). Similar results were found between sub-genomes LF and MF2 (<i>P</i> value = 2.58 × 10<sup>−3</sup>, binomial test) (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#Fig3">3c, d</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#MOESM1">11</a>). Significant expression differences were not found for paralogous genes between MF1 and MF2 (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#MOESM1">11</a>). We used different thresholds to determine the dominant expression status as 1-, 1.5-, 3-, 4-, 6-, and 10-fold changes, and similar results were found (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#Fig3">3b–d</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Sub-genome dominance phenomenon observed in the paleo-allopolyploidy genome of white lupin."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 3: Sub-genome dominance phenomenon observed in the paleo-allopolyploidy genome of white lupin.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-020-14891-z/MediaObjects/41467_2020_14891_Fig3_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-020-14891-z/MediaObjects/41467_2020_14891_Fig3_HTML.png" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="774" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p><b>a</b> The density of syntenic genes in three sub-genomes of <i>L. albus</i> compared to the deduced diploid ancestor of <i>Lupinus</i> (genes of diploid genome <i>P. vulgaris</i> were used as the reference). The <i>x</i>-axis denotes the physical position of each gene locus in the genome of the diploid ancestor. The <i>y</i>-axis denotes the percentage of retained homologous genes in white lupin sub-genomes around each diploid ancestor gene, where 500 genes flanking each side of a certain gene locus were analyzed, giving a total window size of 1001 genes. <b>b</b> The number of dominantly expressed paralogs between sub-genomes LF and MF1, with significantly more genes located at sub-genome LF (red) than their paralogs from sub-genome MF1 (green) under different rules to determine the status of dominant expression. <b>c</b> Similar to <b>b</b>, the number of dominantly expressed paralogs between sub-genomes LF and MF2, with significantly more genes located at sub-genome LF (red) than their paralogs from sub-genome MF2 (blue). <b>d</b> The number of dominantly expressed paralogs between sub-genomes MF1 and MF2; no significant differences were found. <b>e</b> The difference in average TE density in the vicinity regions of paralogous genes from the three sub-genomes of white lupin. <b>f</b> Sub-genomes LF and MF1 with the gene dominantly (<b>d</b>) expressed in LF (dLF). <b>g</b> Sub-genomes LF and MF2 with the gene dominantly expressed in LF. Source data are provided as a Source Data file.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>The density of TE sequences was negatively associated with the dominant expression status of paralogous genes in white lupin. The sub-genome LF had the lowest density of TEs (significant differences in both copy number and sequence density of TEs, Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#MOESM1">12</a>) compared to the two other sub-genomes MF1/2 (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#Fig3">3e</a>). Genes from sub-genome LF that were dominantly expressed had a lower density of TE sequences in their promoter regions (2 kb upstream of the transcription start site) compared to their paralogs (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#Fig3">3f, g</a>). Sub-genome LF had the highest number of gene copies and most genes were expressed at higher levels as well as at lowest TE density. This suggests that <i>L. albus</i> may have experienced a WGT similar to that of allopolyploid species as <i>Brassica</i>, in which a “two-step” polyploidization process resulted in the sub-genome dominance phenomenon<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Cheng, F., Wu, J. &amp; Wang, X. Genome triplication drove the diversification of Brassica plants. Hortic. Res. 1, 14024 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d56465e1242">25</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">P-use efficiency genes were largely expanded in white lupin</h3><p>The expanded genes of <i>L. albus</i> differ from those of <i>L. angustifolius</i>. We compared genes that had two or more paralogous copies to genes that had only one copy in white lupin. The Gene Ontology (GO) terms GO:0003700 (transcription factor activity), GO:0006412 (translation), GO:0005840 (ribosome), and GO:0005524 (ATP binding) were over-represented in these multiple-copy genes (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#MOESM1">1</a>). Genes annotated with these GO term functions were over-retained after gene fractionation (loss) following WGT, which is consistent with the theory of gene dosage balance<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Birchler, J. A. &amp; Veitia, R. A. Gene balance hypothesis: connecting issues of dosage sensitivity across biological disciplines. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 109, 14746–14753 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d56465e1263">26</a></sup>. Furthermore, genes with GO terms GO:0006817 (phosphate transport), GO:0016036 (cellular response to phosphate starvation), and GO:0006096 (glycolysis), GO:0006099 (tricarboxylic acid (TCA) cycle), GO:0008610 (lipid biosynthetic process), and GO:0048364 (root development), as well as GO:0048829 (root cap development) were also over-retained in white lupin (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#MOESM5">1</a>), indicating that genes involved in P homeostasis and acquisition are under selection to be retained through gene fractionation. We compared the copy number difference of orthologous genes between <i>L. albus</i> and <i>L. angustifolius</i>. The two species shared a common WGT event and diverged over ~4 Mya. These two species show differences in the composition of expanded genes. Genes annotated as “glycolysis,” “tricarboxylic acid cycle,” “lipid biosynthetic process,” “ATPase activity,” and different types of phosphate-related functions increased in <i>L. albus</i> compared to <i>L. angustifolius</i> (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#MOESM5">2</a>). This indicates that over-retaining of genes in these pathways occurred in <i>L. albus</i> after it diverged from <i>L. angustifolius</i>.</p><p>The number of P-use efficiency (PUE) genes has increased through WGT, tandem, and dispersed duplications in white lupin. For genes generated through WGT, we found that GO terms related to the phosphate biology process and root development were enriched in over-retained genes (having more than one copy of paralogs) in both <i>L. albus</i> and <i>L. angustifolius</i> (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#MOESM5">3</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#MOESM5">4</a>). For the tandem duplicated genes, GO terms such as “phosphate transport,” “ATPase activity,” “lipid transport,” and “root hair cell differentiation” were enriched in <i>L. albus</i> (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#MOESM5">5</a>), but not in <i>L. angustifolius</i> (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#MOESM5">6</a>). For genes grouped into “dispersed duplication,” many PUE-related GO terms such as “phosphate transport” and “cellular response to phosphate starvation” were enriched in <i>L. albus</i>, but not in <i>L. angustifolius</i> (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#MOESM5">7</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#MOESM5">8</a>). These results suggest that the high use efficiency of P developed along with genes amplified through WGT in <i>Lupinus</i> and further expanded through tandem and dispersed duplications in <i>L. albus</i> after its divergence from <i>L. angustifolius</i>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec7">Adaptive pathways of white lupin to low-P availability</h3><p>P deficiency induces a low-P adaptive response in white lupin. We performed mRNA-seq analysis on leaves, stems, and roots of white lupin under P-deficient (−P) and P-sufficient (+P) conditions (see Methods). Cluster roots from the −P condition were further dissected into four parts: the pre-emergent zone (PE, 2–3 cm adjacent to the root tips of first-order laterals), young cluster roots (YCRs), mature cluster roots (MCRs), and old cluster roots (OCRs) (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#MOESM1">10</a>). We identified a total of 12,380 genes differentially expressed in leaves, stems, and different types of root tissues under P deficiency compared to those under +P conditions (|log<sub>2</sub> fold change| ≥ 1; FDR (false discovery rate)-adjusted <i>P</i> value &lt; 0.01) (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#MOESM1">11</a>). Mfuzz clustering and GO enrichment analyses determined that these low-P-induced genes have a strong enrichment in GO terms associated with lipid metabolic process, carbohydrate metabolic process, and transport, as well as cell cycle and motor activity (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#MOESM1">12</a>), showing a classical phosphate-starvation response that is consistent with previous studies in <i>L. albus</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Secco, D., Shou, H., Whelan, J. &amp; Berkowitz, O. RNA-seq analysis identifies an intricate regulatory network controlling cluster root development in white lupin. BMC Genomics 15, 230 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d56465e1369">27</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="O’Rourke, J. A. et al. An RNA-Seq transcriptome analysis of orthophosphate-deficient white lupin reveals novel insights into phosphorus acclimation in plants. Plant Physiol. 161, 705 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d56465e1372">28</a></sup> and <i>A</i>. <i>thaliana</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Wu, P. et al. Phosphate starvation triggers distinct alterations of genome expression in Arabidopsis roots and leaves. Plant Physiol. 132, 1260 (2003)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d56465e1381">29</a></sup>.</p><p>There are 512 genes that function in low-P response pathways referred to the previous studies in <i>A. thaliana</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Vance, C. P., Uhde‐Stone, C. &amp; Allan, D. L. Phosphorus acquisition and use: critical adaptations by plants for securing a nonrenewable resource. N. Phytol. 157, 423–447 (2003)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d56465e1390">3</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Wu, P. et al. Phosphate starvation triggers distinct alterations of genome expression in Arabidopsis roots and leaves. Plant Physiol. 132, 1260 (2003)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d56465e1393">29</a></sup> and white lupin<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Secco, D., Shou, H., Whelan, J. &amp; Berkowitz, O. RNA-seq analysis identifies an intricate regulatory network controlling cluster root development in white lupin. BMC Genomics 15, 230 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d56465e1397">27</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="O’Rourke, J. A. et al. An RNA-Seq transcriptome analysis of orthophosphate-deficient white lupin reveals novel insights into phosphorus acclimation in plants. Plant Physiol. 161, 705 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d56465e1400">28</a></sup>. Thus, we identified 882 putative orthologs of these 512 genes and termed them as PUE genes (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#MOESM5">9</a>–<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#MOESM5">11</a>). These PUE genes show similar patterns of biased distribution and dominant expression to that of the sub-genome level. More than 40% of these PUE genes in white lupin (363 out of 882) showed two-fold changes of gene expression in the −P treatment (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#Fig4">4</a>, Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#MOESM1">13</a>, and Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#MOESM5">12</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#MOESM5">13</a>). More importantly, consistent results were found compared to previous transcriptome studies in <i>L. albus</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Secco, D., Shou, H., Whelan, J. &amp; Berkowitz, O. RNA-seq analysis identifies an intricate regulatory network controlling cluster root development in white lupin. BMC Genomics 15, 230 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d56465e1425">27</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="O’Rourke, J. A. et al. An RNA-Seq transcriptome analysis of orthophosphate-deficient white lupin reveals novel insights into phosphorus acclimation in plants. Plant Physiol. 161, 705 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d56465e1428">28</a></sup>, including up-regulated genes—under P deficiency—that encode sulfoquinovosyltransferase and monogalactosyl diacylglycerol synthase, which are involved in remodeling membrane lipid composition most probably to remobilize P from phospholipids<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Cheng, L. et al. White lupin cluster root acclimation to phosphorus deficiency and root hair development involve unique glycerophosphodiester phosphodiesterases. Plant Physiol. 156, 1131–1148 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d56465e1432">30</a></sup> as well as mitochondrial malate dehydrogenase, aconitase, and phosphate transporter, which are involved in carbon metabolism and phosphate transport (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#Fig4">4</a>). The differentiation in transcription levels of these PUE genes was further supported by evidence from physiological datasets (Supplementary Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#MOESM1">13</a>–<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#MOESM1">16</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="Expression profiles of the differentially expressed P-use efficiency genes of white lupin under P-sufficient or P-deficient conditions."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 4: Expression profiles of the differentially expressed P-use efficiency genes of white lupin under P-sufficient or P-deficient conditions.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-020-14891-z/MediaObjects/41467_2020_14891_Fig4_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-020-14891-z/MediaObjects/41467_2020_14891_Fig4_HTML.png" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" width="685" height="537" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p>PUE, P-use efficiency; +P, P-sufficient condition; −P, P-deficient condition. Roots from the P-deficient condition were further dissected into normal roots (NRs) and four parts of cluster roots based on the developmental stages, including pre-emergent zone (PE, 2–3 cm behind the root tip of first-order laterals), young cluster roots (YCRs), mature cluster roots (MCRs), and old cluster roots (OCRs). Nine clusters were identified by Mfuzz clustering analysis. Functional categorization of the clusters containing genes with the higher expression in leaves, PE, YCRs, and MCRs under P deficiency is shown on the left. Several key homologs of the <i>A. thaliana</i> PUE genes are given on the right. <i>SQD</i>, sulfoquinovosyltransferase; <i>GDPD</i>, glycerophosphodiester phosphodiesterase; <i>MGD</i>, monogalactosyl diacylglycerol synthase; <i>ABCG36</i>, ATP-binding cassette G36; <i>LBD</i>, lateral organ boundaries domain; <i>PLT</i>, plethora; <i>ACO</i>, aconitase; <i>MMDH</i>, mitochondrial malate dehydrogenase; <i>CSY</i>, citrate synthase; <i>FUM</i>, fumarase; <i>PHT</i>, phosphate transporter; <i>PAP</i>, purple acid phosphatase.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>The low-P adaption in white lupin was found to be associated with duplications of the gene <i>PAP10</i> (purple acid phosphatase 10). PUE genes involved in the TCA cycle, glycolysis, lipid remodeling, phosphate transport, and auxin-regulated root development were expanded and showed a biased retention of paralogs in <i>L. albus</i> compared to <i>M. truncatula</i> and <i>L. angustifolius</i> (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#MOESM5">11</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#MOESM5">14</a>). In particular, <i>PAP</i> genes, which are important for organic P mobilization in cluster roots of white lupin<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Tran, H. T., Hurley, B. A. &amp; Plaxton, W. C. Feeding hungry plants: the role of purple acid phosphatases in phosphate nutrition. Plant Sci. 179, 14–27 (2010)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d56465e1521">31</a></sup>, were largely expanded in the white lupin genome. White lupin has eight copies of <i>PAP10</i> compared to only one copy in <i>A. thaliana</i>. Among the eight <i>PAP10</i> genes, three, three, and two copies have arisen from polyploydization, tandem, and dispersed duplications, respectively (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#MOESM1">17</a> and Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#MOESM5">15</a>). Peak transcript abundance of <i>PAP10</i> genes coincides with the increase in acid phosphatase (AP) activity that occurs in cluster roots in −P plants (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#Fig4">4</a> and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#MOESM1">18</a>). To further investigate the roles of <i>PAPs</i> in white lupin adaptation to low-P availability, we generated <i>PAP10</i> (<i>Lal_00013458</i>) and <i>PAP12</i> (<i>Lal_00004477</i>, ortholog of <i>A. thaliana PAP12</i>) (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#MOESM1">14</a>) overexpressing hairy roots in white lupin using the <i>Agrobacterium rhizogenes</i>-based transformation system. When phytate (main organic P in soil) was the only P source in 1/2 Murashige and Skoog basal media, <i>35S:PAP10</i> and <i>35S:PAP12</i> transgenic hairy roots were found to contain more P per gram of dry weight than those transformed with empty vectors (Supplementary Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#MOESM1">19</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#MOESM1">20</a>), supporting the function of multi-copy <i>PAP10</i> genes in releasing P from external organic P esters.</p><p>Besides these 882 PUE genes determined as aforementioned, we further found <i>LaABCG36</i>s and <i>LaABCG37</i>s as the potential genes functioning in the cluster root formation through auxin modulation in white lupin. Auxin is involved in P deficiency-induced formation of cluster roots in white lupin. Variation of indole-3-acetic acid (IAA) contents in different stages of the cluster roots was observed (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#Fig5">5a</a>). Furthermore, the auxin influx inhibitor 3-chloro-4-hydroxyphenylacetic acid (CHPAA) and efflux inhibitor 1-naphtuylphthalamic acid (NPA) treatments resulted in a substantial decline in cluster root number under P deficiency conditions (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#Fig5">5b</a>), which is consistent with a previous report<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Xu, W. et al. Abscisic acid accumulation modulates auxin transport in the root tip to enhance proton secretion for maintaining root growth under moderate water stress. N. Phytol. 197, 139–150 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d56465e1606">32</a></sup>. Ethylenediaminetetraacetic acid (EDTA), a metal chelating agent, can also inhibit the formation of cluster roots under −P hydroponic solution (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#Fig5">5c</a>). The activity of AAA proteins (ATPases Associated with diverse cellular Activities) can be inhibited by EDTA<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Xu, X. et al. The major-effect quantitative trait locus Cs ARN 6.1 encodes an AAA ATP ase domain-containing protein that is associated with waterlogging stress tolerance by promoting adventitious root formation. Plant J. 93, 917–930 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d56465e1614">33</a></sup>. Members of the G subgroup of the ATP-binding cassette transporter, <i>ABCG36</i>, and its homolog, <i>ABCG37</i>, contain two AAA domains. The two genes act redundantly to transport the auxin precursor IBA (indole-3-butyric acid) in <i>A. thaliana</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Strader, L. C. et al. Multiple facets of Arabidopsis seedling development require indole-3-butyric acid–derived auxin. Plant Cell 23, 984 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d56465e1626">34</a></sup>. This leads to fluctuation of the level of auxin that is required for the development of lateral roots in <i>A. thaliana</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Strader, L. C. et al. Multiple facets of Arabidopsis seedling development require indole-3-butyric acid–derived auxin. Plant Cell 23, 984 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d56465e1633">34</a></sup>. Here, we determined four orthologous genes (<i>LaABCG36a</i>–<i>d</i>) of <i>ABCG36</i> and three orthologous genes (<i>LaABCG37a</i>–<i>c</i>) of <i>ABCG37</i> in <i>L. albus</i> (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#MOESM1">14</a>), but only two and two, respectively, in <i>P. vulgaris</i> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#Fig5">5d</a>). Four orthologs of <i>ABCG36</i> and two orthologs of <i>ABCG37</i> were expanded through the WGT event, while one ortholog of <i>ABCG37</i> was expanded through tandem duplication in white lupin (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#MOESM1">17</a>). RNA-sequencing analysis showed that <i>LaABCG36s</i> and <i>LaABCG37s</i> had the lowest expression in the PE zone, followed by a continuous expression that increased during the outgrowth and maturation of cluster roots compared with roots under +P conditions (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#Fig5">5e</a>). In addition, phosphoproteomics analysis showed that the relative phosphorylation levels of <i>LaABCG36b</i> and <i>LaABCG36c</i> were increased by about 2.6- and 5.4-fold in white lupin plants under P deficiency compared to those under −P condition (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#MOESM5">16</a>). Altogether, these results strongly suggest that auxin modulation in white lupin is important for cluster root formation by involving two potential genes, <i>LaABCG36s</i> or <i>LaABCG37s</i>.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-5" data-title="Roles of auxin modulation in cluster root formation of white lupin."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig5" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 5: Roles of auxin modulation in cluster root formation of white lupin.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z/figures/5" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-020-14891-z/MediaObjects/41467_2020_14891_Fig5_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-5-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-020-14891-z/MediaObjects/41467_2020_14891_Fig5_HTML.png" alt="figure5" loading="lazy" width="685" height="466" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-5-desc"><p><b>a</b> Quantification of IAA contents (ng per g of fresh weight, ng g<sup>−1</sup> FW) in different types of white lupin roots. <i>n</i> = 3 plants. Effects of auxin polar transport inhibitors (<b>b</b>) and EDTA (<b>c</b>) on the formation of cluster roots in white lupin under P-sufficient (+P) or P-deficient (-P) conditions. <i>n</i> = 4 plants for (<b>b</b>) and <i>n</i> = 3 plants for (<b>c</b>). Error bars indicate s.e.m. <i>P</i> value was calculated using the unpaired two-sided Student’s <i>t</i> test. CHPAA, auxin influx inhibitor 3-chloro-4-hydroxyphenylacetic acid; NPA, auxin efflux inhibitor 1-naphtuylphthalamic acid; EDTA, metal chelator ethylenediaminetetraacetic acid. <b>d</b> Neighbor-joining phylogenetic tree of <i>ABCG36</i> and <i>ABCG37</i> genes from <i>A. thaliana</i>, <i>P. vulgaris</i>, and <i>L. albus</i>. <b>e</b> Expression profiles of <i>LaABCG36a–d</i> and <i>LaABCG37a–c</i> in roots under P-sufficient (+P) condition and normal roots (NRs) or different developmental stages of cluster roots under P deficiency (−P). PE, pre-emergent zone, 2–3 cm behind the root tip of first-order laterals; YCR, young cluster root; MCR, mature cluster root; OCR, old cluster root. The source data underlying Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#MOESM6">5a–c</a> are provided as a Source Data file.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z/figures/5" data-track-dest="link:Figure5 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec8" data-title="Discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec8-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec8">Discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec8-content"><p>White lupin is a dinitrogen (N<sub>2</sub>)-fixing legume. It develops cluster roots that remobilize and efficiently use soil P, and thus is a model crop for studying plant adaption to low-P availability in the soil. Through the assembly and analysis of the genome of white lupin (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#Tab1">1</a> and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#MOESM1">3</a>), we deduced its diploid ancestor before WGT (Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#Fig1">1</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#Fig2">2</a>) and reconstructed three sub-genomes as well as determined sub-genome dominance and biased TE distribution in white lupin (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#Fig3">3</a>). Furthermore, we found that the PUE genes have been largely expanded through WGT, tandem, or dispersed duplications (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#MOESM1">17</a>). Taken together, these results increased our knowledge on the genome evolution, as well as provide clues to the low-P adaptation of white lupin from the genome perspective. Additionally, protein phosphorylation might be involved in the regulation of PUE genes (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#MOESM5">16</a>).</p><p>Four main biological pathways have likely co-evolved as adaptations of white lupin to low levels of P (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#MOESM1">21</a>). The first pathway involves carbon fixation and the supply of carbon to roots<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Cheng, L. et al. Interactions between light intensity and phosphorus nutrition affect the phosphate-mining capacity of white lupin (Lupinus albus L.). J. Exp. Bot. 65, 2995–3003 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d56465e1819">4</a></sup>. White lupin was observed to largely maintain photosynthetic carbon fixation after 4 weeks of P deficiency (Supplementary Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#MOESM1">13</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#MOESM1">14</a>). We found that genes related to sucrose transport were expanded (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#MOESM5">14</a>), such as orthologs (<i>Lal_00013937</i> and <i>Lal_00026769</i>) of <i>AtSWEET7</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Durand, M. et al. Carbon source–sink relationship in Arabidopsis thaliana: the role of sucrose transporters. Planta 247, 587–611 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d56465e1841">35</a></sup>.
