These expanded genes were significantly up-regulated under P deficiency, and they should play an important role in sucrose transport from shoot to root.</p><p>Secondly, we identified genes whose expressions were associated with variation of IAA contents during cluster root formation in white lupin (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#Fig5">5a</a>). These genes are orthologs of two IBA transporter genes <i>ABCG36</i> and <i>ABCG37</i>, which have not been reported as PUE genes in response to P starvation in <i>A. thaliana</i> or white lupin, but are functioning in auxin-regulated adventitious root formation in <i>A</i>. <i>thaliana</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Veloccia, A. et al. Ethylene and auxin interaction in the control of adventitious rooting in Arabidopsis thaliana. J. Exp. Bot. 67, 6445–6458 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d56465e1866">36</a></sup>. White lupin has four and three copies of <i>ABCG36</i> and <i>ABCG37</i>, respectively, compared to only one copy in <i>A</i>. <i>thaliana</i> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#Fig5">5d</a>). These multiple copies of IBA transport genes have mainly arisen from polyploydization duplication (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#MOESM1">17</a>). Moreover, the phosphorylation levels of <i>LaABCG36b</i> and <i>LaABCG36c</i> were up-regulated under P deficiency (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#MOESM5">16</a>). Therefore, the genes <i>LaABCG36s</i> and <i>LaABCG37s</i> were considered as potentially involved in the cluster root formation in white lupin through the regulation of auxin homeostasis.</p><p>Thirdly, bursts of organic acid and AP extrusion in cluster roots were previously observed to be associated with their ability toward external P remobilization from soil<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Uhde-Stone, C. in Legume Nitrogen Fixation in Soils with Low Phosphorus Availability 243–280 (Springer, 2017)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d56465e1908">2</a></sup>. With the genome data, we found that genes involved in glycolysis, TCA cycle, and dark fixation of CO<sub>2</sub>, which are required for the synthesis of organic acids in cluster roots<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Massonneau, A. et al. Metabolic changes associated with cluster root development in white lupin (Lupinus albus L.): relationship between organic acid excretion, sucrose metabolism and energy status. Planta 213, 534–542 (2001)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d56465e1914">37</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Johnson, J. F., Allan, D. L. &amp; Vance, C. P. Phosphorus stress-induced proteoid roots show altered metabolism in Lupinus albus. Plant Physiol. 104, 657 (1994)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d56465e1917">38</a></sup>, were largely expanded in white lupin (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#MOESM5">14</a>). Meanwhile, the increased expression profiles of genes involved in these processes are consistent with reported transcriptome studies in <i>L. albus</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Secco, D., Shou, H., Whelan, J. &amp; Berkowitz, O. RNA-seq analysis identifies an intricate regulatory network controlling cluster root development in white lupin. BMC Genomics 15, 230 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d56465e1926">27</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="O’Rourke, J. A. et al. An RNA-Seq transcriptome analysis of orthophosphate-deficient white lupin reveals novel insights into phosphorus acclimation in plants. Plant Physiol. 161, 705 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d56465e1929">28</a></sup> and are further supported by physiological data (Supplementary Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#MOESM1">13</a>–<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#MOESM1">16</a>). These genes include <i>PEPC</i> (<i>PHOSPHOENOLPYRUVATE CARBOXYLASE</i>) (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#MOESM1">17</a>), which plays important roles in dark fixation of CO<sub>2</sub> and whose protein phosphorylation level was significantly up-regulated under P deficiency (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#MOESM5">13</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#MOESM5">16</a>), as well as <i>PAP</i>, one of the most well-studied groups of phosphatase genes linked to Pi remobilization<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Tran, H. T., Hurley, B. A. &amp; Plaxton, W. C. Feeding hungry plants: the role of purple acid phosphatases in phosphate nutrition. Plant Sci. 179, 14–27 (2010)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d56465e1961">31</a></sup>, whose overexpressing in hairy roots can mobilize P efficiently from organic P esters (Supplementary Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#MOESM1">19</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#MOESM1">20</a>) in white lupin.</p><p>Fourthly, with respect to the reuse of internal P, we found that genes with a higher expression level in leaves under P deficiency (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#Fig4">4</a>) were involved in the remodeling of membrane lipid composition most probably to remobilize P from phospholipids, which should be important to the internal P reuse in white lupin. Finally, it is likely that the copy number expansion and function evolution (such as expression and phosphorylation regulation) of genes involved in the four main pathways have contributed to white lupin’s adaptation to low P.</p><p>In conclusion, P is a limited resource derived from phosphate rock. The ready supply of P will eventually be depleted. Heavy P fertilizer use in farmland can also lead to environmental pollution. The legume white lupin provides an opportunity to study the mechanism of plant adaption to low available soil P. Understanding the high PUE in white lupin may also help to overcome the inhibition of N<sub>2</sub> fixation under low levels of P in other leguminous crops. The dissection of the genome sequences of white lupin provides insights into its genome evolution and low-P adaptation. The results of this study may contribute to the breeding of crops with high PUE traits.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec9" data-title="Methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec9-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec9">Methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec9-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec10">Plant material and genome survey</h3><p>White lupin (<i>L. albus</i>) cv. Amiga was used for genome sequencing. Seeds were provided by Professor Feng Yan at the Justus Liebig University, Germany. Lupin plants were grown hydroponically in a +P or −P nutrient solution as described by Yan et al.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Yan, F., Zhu, Y., Muller, C., Zorb, C. &amp; Schubert, S. Adaptation of H+-pumping and plasma membrane H+ ATPase activity in proteoid roots of white lupin under phosphate deficiency. Plant Physiol. 129, 50–63 (2002)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d56465e1998">39</a></sup> and were then moved to a greenhouse. If not otherwise specified, 5-week-old plants were used for the measurements in this study.</p><p>DNA was isolated from plants using a standard magnetic beads genomic DNA extraction method. Data from 60 Gb Illumina Solexa 150 bp paired-end reads (350 bp insert library) were generated and the genome was estimated using a K-mer size of 17 bp by JellyFish and GenomeScope<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Marcais, G. &amp; Kingsford, C. A fast, lock-free approach for efficient parallel counting of occurrences of k-mers. Bioinformatics 27, 764–770 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d56465e2005">40</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Vurture, G. W. et al. GenomeScope: fast reference-free genome profiling from short reads. Bioinformatics 33, 2202–2204 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d56465e2008">41</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec11">Genome sequencing and assembly</h3><p>Approximately 84.29 Gb PacBio sequencing data were generated, covering about 140-fold of the estimated genome size of white lupin. The average size of the PacBio reads was ~8.5 kb, and there were more than 80.67 Gb data with reads longer than 3 kb. The genome assembler Canu<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Koren, S. et al. Canu: scalable and accurate long-read assembly via adaptive k-mer weighting and repeat separation. Genome Res. 27, 722–736 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d56465e2020">14</a></sup> was used for PacBio reads correction, trimming, and assembly following the standard pipeline. The following parameters were used in the assembly: minReadLength = 1000, GenomeSize = 0.7 g, minOverlapLength = 500 bp, and corOutCoverage = 200. The assembly graphs were checked by Bandage<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Wick, R. R., Schultz, M. B., Zobel, J. &amp; Holt, K. E. Bandage: interactive visualization of de novo genome assemblies. Bioinformatics 31, 3350–3352 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d56465e2024">42</a></sup> to estimate the assembly quality on the heterozygous, homologous, and repetitive regions. Finally, 3171 contigs were obtained (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#Tab1">1</a>). These contigs were further polished with ~60× coverage of Illumina Solexa 150 bp paired-end reads to remove sequencing errors using Pilon<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Utturkar, S. M., Klingeman, D. M., Hurt, R. A. Jr. &amp; Brown, S. D. A case study into microbial genome assembly gap sequences and finishing strategies. Front. Microbiol. 8, 1272 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d56465e2031">15</a></sup> and Bowtie2<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Langdon, W. B. Performance of genetic programming optimised Bowtie2 on genome comparison and analytic testing (GCAT) benchmarks. BioData Min. 8, 1 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d56465e2035">43</a></sup>.</p><p>The Hi-C library<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="van Berkum, N. L. et al. Hi-C: a method to study the three-dimensional architecture of genomes. J. Vis. Exp. 6, e1869 (2010)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d56465e2042">16</a></sup> was constructed using the <i>Hin</i>dIII enzyme with fresh leaf tissue of white lupin by the Proximo Hi-C Plant Kit following standard protocols, and 288 M of 150 bp paired-end reads were generated on the Illumina HiSeq2000 platform. These Hi-C reads were mapped to the assembled contigs using Bowtie2<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Langdon, W. B. Performance of genetic programming optimised Bowtie2 on genome comparison and analytic testing (GCAT) benchmarks. BioData Min. 8, 1 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d56465e2049">43</a></sup> (BOWTIE2_GLOBAL_OPTIONS = –very-sensitive -L 30 –score-min L,−0.6,−0.2 –end-to-end –reorder; BOWTIE2_LOCAL_OPTIONS = –very-sensitive -L 20 –score-min L,−0.6,−0.2 –end-to-end –reorder), and 38.16% valid mapping reads were obtained. These mapped datasets were submitted to Juicer<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Durand, N. C. et al. Juicer provides a one-click system for analyzing loop-resolution Hi-C experiments. Cell Syst. 3, 95–98 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d56465e2053">44</a></sup> software and 3ddna<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Dudchenko, O. et al. De novo assembly of the Aedes aegypti genome using Hi-C yields chromosome-length scaffolds. Science 356, 92–95 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d56465e2057">45</a></sup> for grouping, ordering, and orienting (scaffolding) of the contigs. The linking results were also manually curated to correct mis-joins and mis-assemblies based on visualization with JuicerBox<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Durand, N. C. et al. Juicebox provides a visualization system for Hi-C Contact maps with unlimited zoom. Cell Syst. 3, 99–101 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d56465e2062">46</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec12">Repetitive elements’ prediction</h3><p>The de novo repeat library of white lupin was constructed using the pipeline of RepeatModeler<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Tarailo-Graovac, M. &amp; Chen, N. Using RepeatMasker to identify repetitive elements in genomic sequences. Curr. Protoc. Bioinform. 4, 10 (2009)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d56465e2074">20</a></sup>. TE families with lengths longer than 100 bp and N content &lt;5% were selected, and we identified 10,550 candidate TE families as the TE library. TE sequences in the genome of white lupin were further identified using homology-based searches against both Repbase and the de novo TE library. At the DNA level, TEs were identified using RepeatMasker (<a href="http://www.repeatmasker.org/">http://www.repeatmasker.org/</a>), and at the protein level, TEs were identified using RepeatProteinMask<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Tarailo-Graovac, M. &amp; Chen, N. Using RepeatMasker to identify repetitive elements in genomic sequences. Curr. Protoc. Bioinform. 4, 10 (2009)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d56465e2082">20</a></sup>. The results were combined, and redundant TE elements that overlapped (≥80%) to other ones were filtered out.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec13">Protein-coding gene prediction and annotation</h3><p>After pre-masking of TE sequences, genes were predicted via ab initio prediction, homology-based searches, and mRNA-seq-assisted prediction. Augustus<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Stanke, M., Tzvetkova, A. &amp; Morgenstern, B. AUGUSTUS at EGASP: using EST, protein and genomic alignments for improved gene prediction in the human genome. Genome Biol. 7, 1–8 (2006). S11." href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d56465e2094">47</a></sup> and GeneScan<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Burge, C. &amp; Karlin, S. Prediction of complete gene structures in human genomic DNA. J. Mol. Biol. 268, 78–94 (1997)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d56465e2098">48</a></sup> were used in ab initio gene prediction. In homology searches, we collected protein sequences of <i>Arabidopsis thalina</i>, <i>P. vulgaris</i>, <i>Cajanus cajan</i>, <i>L. angustifolius</i>, <i>G. max</i>, <i>C. arietinum</i>, <i>Pyunus persica</i>, <i>M. truncatula</i>, <i>A. duranensis</i>, <i>Vigan angularis</i>, and <i>Lotus japonicas</i>, and used Genewise v2.4.1<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Birney, E., Clamp, M. &amp; Durbin, R. GeneWise and genomewise. Genome Res. 14, 988–995 (2004)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d56465e2137">49</a></sup> software. We used mRNA-seq datasets on tissues of root, leaf, and stem of white lupin to assist gene prediction. Total RNA was isolated using TRIzol reagent (Invitrogen) according to the manufacturer’s instructions. An independent mRNA-seq library was prepared by the Beijing Genomics Institute (Shenzhen, China), and sequencing was performed on the BGISEQ500 platform (Illumina). Hisat2, Stringtie, and TransDecode<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Haas, B. J. et al. De novo transcript sequence reconstruction from RNA-seq using the Trinity platform for reference generation and analysis. Nat. Protoc. 8, 1494–1512 (2013)." href="#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d56465e2141">50</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Kim, D., Langmead, B. &amp; Salzberg, S. L. HISAT: a fast spliced aligner with low memory requirements. Nat. Methods 12, 357–360 (2015)." href="#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d56465e2141_1">51</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Pertea, M. et al. StringTie enables improved reconstruction of a transcriptome from RNA-seq reads. Nat. Biotechnol. 33, 290–295 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d56465e2144">52</a></sup> were used to assemble mRNA-seq data to unigenes and to perform gene prediction. All gene prediction datasets were combined by Maker<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Cantarel, B. L. et al. MAKER: an easy-to-use annotation pipeline designed for emerging model organism genomes. Genome Res. 18, 188–196 (2008)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d56465e2148">53</a></sup> to generate the final gene set of white lupin.</p><p>The predicted genes of white lupin were further aligned to the Swiss-Prot and TrEMBL databases<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="O’Donovan, C. et al. High-quality protein knowledge resource: SWISS-PROT and TrEMBL. Brief. Bioinform. 3, 275–284 (2002)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d56465e2155">54</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Boutet, E., Lieberherr, D., Tognolli, M., Schneider, M. &amp; Bairoch, A. UniProtKB/Swiss-Prot. Methods Mol. Biol. 406, 89–112 (2007)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d56465e2158">55</a></sup> with BLASTP at an <i>E</i> value 1 × 10<sup>−5</sup>, and the most significant hits were retained. InterPro<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="Hunter, S. et al. InterPro: the integrative protein signature database. Nucleic Acids Res. 37, D211–D215 (2009)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d56465e2167">56</a></sup> was used to annotate motifs and domains in predicted genes by comparison with Pfam, PRINTS, PROSITE, ProDom, and SMART<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Corpet, F., Gouzy, J. &amp; Kahn, D. The ProDom database of protein domain families. Nucleic Acids Res. 26, 323–326 (1998)." href="#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d56465e2171">57</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Attwood, T. K. The PRINTS database: a resource for identification of protein families. Brief. Bioinform. 3, 252–263 (2002)." href="#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d56465e2171_1">58</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Hulo, N. et al. The PROSITE database. Nucleic Acids Res. 34, D227–D230 (2006)." href="#ref-CR59" id="ref-link-section-d56465e2171_2">59</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 60" title="Finn, R. D. et al. Pfam: the protein families database. Nucleic Acids Res. 42, D222–D230 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d56465e2174">60</a></sup> databases. Additionally, the white lupin genes were mapped to KEGG (Kyoto Encyclopedia of Genes and Genomes) pathway maps<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 61" title="Kanehisa, M. The KEGG database. Novartis Found. Symp. 247, 91–101 (2002). discussion 101–103, 119–128, 244–252." href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#ref-CR61" id="ref-link-section-d56465e2179">61</a></sup> based on the most probable Swiss-Prot hit for each gene.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec14">Identification of syntenic genes</h3><p>The syntenic orthologs of <i>L. albus</i> genes in <i>P. vulgaris</i> and other legume species were determined by both sequence similarity and the sequence homozygy of their flanking genes using SynOrths<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Cheng, F., Wu, J., Fang, L. &amp; Wang, X. Syntenic gene analysis between Brassica rapa and other Brassicaceae species. Front. Plant Sci. 3, 198 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d56465e2198">21</a></sup> with <i>L. albus</i> as the query genome and each of the other legume species as the subject genome. <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i>, a member of the Brassicaceae, is highly divergent from legume species. Genome synteny between <i>A. thaliana</i> and legume species was largely absent. Therefore, we determined orthologous genes (only sequence homology, rather than genomic synteny) between <i>A. thaliana</i> and legume species.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec15"><i>K</i>
<sub>s</sub> and phylogenetic analysis</h3><p>Protein sequences from <i>L. albus</i> were aligned with the syntenic orthologs from 16 legume genomes (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#MOESM1">6</a>) using MUSCLE<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 62" title="Edgar, R. C. MUSCLE: multiple sequence alignment with high accuracy and high throughput. Nucleic Acids Res. 32, 1792–1797 (2004)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#ref-CR62" id="ref-link-section-d56465e2234">62</a></sup>. Protein alignments were translated into coding sequence alignments using an in-house Perl script. <i>K</i><sub>s</sub> values were then calculated based on the coding sequence alignments using the method of Nei and Gojobori as implemented in the KaKs_calculator<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 63" title="Zhang, Z. et al. KaKs_Calculator: calculating Ka and Ks through model selection and model averaging. Genomics Proteom. Bioinform. 4, 259–263 (2006)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#ref-CR63" id="ref-link-section-d56465e2242">63</a></sup>. The <i>K</i><sub>s</sub> values of all syntenic orthologs between <i>L. albus</i> and legume species were plotted as histograms (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#Fig1">1b</a>). For the phylogenetic analysis, the MUSCLE alignments performed in the <i>K</i><sub>s</sub> analysis were used. The genotypes of 78,926 synonymous loci were extracted from multiple alignments on each of these 1664 syntenic orthologs shared by all 16 legume species. The 78,926 synonymous loci were concatenated into a single sequence for each species, and a phylogenetic tree was constructed with the concatenated sequences using the neighbor-joining method and plotted with the MEGA software (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#Fig1">1a</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec16">Analysis of WGT</h3><p><i>K</i><sub>s</sub> values were calculated for the homologous gene pairs within <i>L. albus</i> and between <i>L. albus</i> and the other legume species. The greatest <i>K</i><sub>s</sub> value for white lupin peanut (<i>K</i><sub>s</sub> ~=0.52) revealed the divergence time of these two species, while the peak for white lupin soybean (<i>K</i><sub>s</sub> ~=0.51) separated the <i>Lupinus</i> lineage-specific WGT event (<i>K</i><sub>s</sub> ~=0.28) from α WGD (<i>K</i><sub>s</sub> ~=0.11) shared by <i>G. max</i> and <i>G. soja</i> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#Fig1">1a</a>). The neutral mutation rate <i>ρ</i> or the constant number 6.38 × 10<sup>−9</sup> was applied to calculate the time of divergence<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Hane, J. K. et al. A comprehensive draft genome sequence for lupin (Lupinus angustifolius), an emerging health food: insights into plant-microbe interactions and legume evolution. Plant Biotechnol. J. 15, 318–330 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d56465e2321">6</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec17">Determination of syntenic fragments</h3><p>Continuously distributed syntenic gene pairs along chromosomes in two genomes were identified as intact ancestral fragments that were inherited by the two species. Factors such as local structural variations and assembly incompetence may exist in either or both genomes. Therefore, local syntenic gene pairs may not be distributed immediately adjacent to others, although they are located in a synteny fragment. We thus determined that if genes from two pairs of syntenic genes were interrupted by &lt;50 genes (inserted in between) or had a distance &lt;200 kb in both genomes, then the two gene pairs were grouped in one syntenic fragment. We performed the analysis between <i>L. albus</i> and <i>P. vulgaris</i>, as well as between <i>L. angustifolius</i> and <i>P. vulgaris</i> using the same method (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#Fig1">1c, d</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec18">Definition of GBs in the Fabaceae</h3><p>We selected three genomes (<i>L. albus</i>, <i>L. angustifolius</i>, and <i>P. vulgaris</i>) for detailed genomic structure comparisons. The diploid genome of <i>P. vulgaris</i> was used as the reference to determine its arrangement of genomic fragments to <i>Lupinus</i>, since it shows reasonable divergence from <i>Lupinus</i> while possessing good genomic synteny to <i>Lupinus</i> genomes (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#Fig2">2</a>). Syntenic gene pairs were used as painting primers in comparative chromosome painting<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 64" title="Mandakova, T. &amp; Lysak, M. A. Chromosomal phylogeny and karyotype evolution in x=7 crucifer species (Brassicaceae). Plant Cell 20, 2559–2570 (2008)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#ref-CR64" id="ref-link-section-d56465e2381">64</a></sup> analysis to determine the relationships of shared genomic fragments between species. We then scanned across the 11 chromosomes of <i>P. vulgaris</i> to find the breakpoints occurring simultaneous to the paralogous fragments in the three sub-genomes of white lupin. Finally, a framework of 26 GBs was built for the comparative genomic analysis in <i>Lupinus</i> and other legume species.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec19">Deciphering the ancestral diploid genome of <i>Lupinus</i>
</h3><p>The GB information was mapped from <i>P. vulgaris</i> to the three sub-genomes of white lupin (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#MOESM1">7</a>). We then searched the GB associations in the genome of <i>L. albus</i> and compared these with the GB associations in <i>P. vulgaris</i>. For the GB associations not existing in <i>P. vulgaris</i> but appearing in more than one sub-genome of <i>L. albus</i>, we counted and recorded these as GB associations that are specific to the diploid ancestor of <i>L. albus</i> (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#MOESM1">8</a>). The same analysis was also performed between <i>L. angustifolius</i> and <i>P. vulgaris</i>, and similar results were obtained. With these GB associations and their orders in the genome of white lupin, we deduced that the diploid ancestor genome of white lupin had nine chromosomes (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#Fig2">2c</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec20">Reconstruction of three sub-genomes in white lupin</h3><p>The white lupin genome is composed of three sub-genomes, each containing 26 GBs. We annotated these GBs along the 25 chromosomes of white lupin. Using the deduced nine chromosomes of the diploid ancestor as the reference, the triplicated copies of each diploid chromosome in the hexapolyploid ancestor (before rearrangement) of white lupin were reconstructed (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#MOESM1">9</a>). Two rules were followed in the linking of genomic fragments: (1) individual chromosome copies could not contain overlapping and redundant genomic fragments, and (2) the final reconstructed optimum sub-genomes were the ones involving the fewest genomic rearrangements compared to the other possibilities.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec21">Comparison of dominant expression between gene doublets</h3><p>The mRNA-seq datasets from three tissues (root, stem, and leaf) were used in this analysis. Reads were mapped to genes of white lupin using Hisat2<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Kim, D., Langmead, B. &amp; Salzberg, S. L. HISAT: a fast spliced aligner with low memory requirements. Nat. Methods 12, 357–360 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d56465e2457">51</a></sup>, and FeatureCount was used to extract mapped reads for each gene to compute the TPM values as the expression levels of the genes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 65" title="Liao, Y., Smyth, G. K. &amp; Shi, W. The Subread aligner: fast, accurate and scalable read mapping by seed-and-vote. Nucleic Acids Res. 41, e108 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#ref-CR65" id="ref-link-section-d56465e2461">65</a></sup>. Paralogous gene pairs (gene doublets) were extracted from the homologous genes among the three sub-genomes of white lupin. To avoid the effects of false mapped reads, all genes were sorted from high to low based on their expression levels. The bottom 1% of genes were considered as not expressed. When comparing the dominant expression status in each gene doublet, at least one of the two genes had an expression value &gt;5.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec22">TE distribution in neighboring regions of white lupin genes</h3><p>We used a 100 bp sliding window with a 10 bp step moving across the 5′ and 3′ flanking regions of genes to estimate the TE density around each gene of white lupin. In each 100 bp window, we calculated the ratio of TE nucleotides and then averaged the ratio across subsets of the white lupin genes. The averaged values were plotted as the TE density in the flanking region of these subsets of white lupin genes (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#Fig3">3e–g</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec23">Gene retention after WGT in white lupin</h3><p>We counted the number of syntenic genes in <i>L. albus</i> in relation to each <i>P. vulgaris</i> gene. There were 7598, 6227, and 5567 <i>P. vulgaris</i> genes that had one, two, and three paralogs in the three sub-genomes of white lupin, respectively. Using the GO for each <i>P. vulgaris</i> gene, we then investigated the gene retention features in <i>L. albus</i>. For each GO term, we compared its enrichment in genes with two or more copies to genes with only one copy in white lupin. The one-sided Fisher’s exact tests tests were performed to check the significance of GO enrichment. Similar analyses were performed between different gene groups (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#MOESM5">1</a>–<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#MOESM5">8</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec24">Genes expanded through different ways of duplications</h3><p>Three main types of duplicated genes were separated in white lupin. Genes having syntenic relationships with any of the 16 legume species (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#MOESM1">6</a>) were considered as either being inherited or expanded through polyploidization (WGT) in white lupin. We then determined neighboring genes with sequence homology to each other in the genome of white lupin as being expanded through tandem duplication. Genes that were not classified into WGT or tandem duplication were grouped as transposition or other ways of duplication and were termed as dispersed duplication genes.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec25">Response of white lupin to P deficiency</h3><p>Leaves, stems, and roots of white lupin under −P and +P hydroponic solution were harvested after 28 days of culture. Roots from the −P condition were further dissected into normal roots and four parts of cluster roots based on the developmental stages, the PE (2–3 cm behind the root tip of first-order laterals), YCRs, MCRs, and OCRs (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#MOESM1">10</a>). Three biological replications were used for each experiment. The processes of mRNA extraction, sequencing, and gene expression level calculation followed the BGI standard methods. Differential expression analysis between −P and +P conditions was performed using DESeq package<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 66" title="Wang, L., Feng, Z., Wang, X., Wang, X. &amp; Zhang, X. DEGseq: an R package for identifying differentially expressed genes from RNA-seq data. Bioinformatics 26, 136–138 (2010)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#ref-CR66" id="ref-link-section-d56465e2528">66</a></sup> (v.1.10.1) in R (v.3.6.1). The resulting <i>P</i> values (negative binomial distribution) were adjusted using Benjamini and Hochberg’s approach for controlling the FDR. Genes with |log<sub>2</sub> fold change)| &gt;1 and FDR value &lt; 0.01 were defined as differentially expressed. The expression dynamics of PUE genes in white lupin were visualized using the “ComplexHeatmap” R package (v.2.2.0).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec26">Identification of PUE genes in white lupin</h3><p>To study the high PUE genes in white lupin, genes involved in PUE-related pathways (e.g., TCA cycle, glycolysis, CO<sub>2</sub> fixation, and root development) from <i>A. thaliana</i> (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#MOESM5">9</a>) were used as subject sequences. All protein sequences of <i>M. truncatula</i> and <i>L. angustifolius</i> and white lupin were aligned to these PUE proteins by Blastp with &gt;1 × 10<sup>−20</sup> <i>E</i> value, &gt;50% coverage, and &gt;35% identity in sequence alignment. The syntenic relationship of these genes in white lupin to <i>M. truncatula</i> or <i>P. vulgaris</i> were checked for confirming the WGT events.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec27">Phosphoproteomic analysis</h3><p>Proteins from roots were extracted by TCA/acetone mixture. After protein quantification, phosphopeptides were enriched by TiO<sub>2</sub> beads. The enriched phosphopeptides were then separated by UltiMate 3000 HPLC (high-performance liquid chromatography) (Thermo Fisher Scientific, San Jose, CA, USA). After a precursor scan of intact peptides was measured in the LTQ Orbitrap Velos (Thermo Fisher Scientific) by scanning from <i>m/z</i> 350−1500 (with a resolution of 120,000), the eight most intense multiple-charged precursor ions were subjected to collision induced for 30 ms with a normalized collision energy of 35.0. Automatic gain control targets were 10,000 for tandem mass spectrometry (MS/MS) scans and 100,000 ions for Orbitrap scans. Data were analyzed using Progenesis LC-MS software (v4.1, Nonlinear Dynamics), and label-free quantification was performed using a liquid chromatography-MS/MS system<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 67" title="Luo, R. et al. Label-free quantitative phosphoproteomic analysis reveals differentially regulated proteins and pathway in PRRSV-infected pulmonary alveolar macrophages. J. Proteome Res. 13, 1270–1280 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#ref-CR67" id="ref-link-section-d56465e2585">67</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec28">IAA analysis</h3><p>Root homogenate was prepared with cold buffer (methanol:H<sub>2</sub>O:acetic acid = 80:20:1, v/v/v). After purification by petroleum ether and ethyl acetate, IAA was quantified by HPLC (Rigol L3000, RIGOL Technologies, Inc. China) with a reverse-phase C18 Gemini HPLC column (250 mm × 4.6 mm, 5 μm). The mobile phase consisted of an equal volume mixture of methanol and acetic acid at a flow rate of 0.8 mL min<sup>−1</sup>. Excitation and emission wavelengths were set at 275 and 345 nm, respectively. The amount of IAA in the sample was calculated from the peak area (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#Fig5">5a</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec29">Addition of cluster root formation inhibitors</h3><p>To evaluate effects of EDTA on cluster root formation, lupin plants grown in +P or −P nutrient solution were treated by 10 mM EDTA for 7 days, while control plants were hydroponically grown without EDTA. In terms of auxin influx and efflux inhibitor (CHPAA and NPA) treatment, 30 µM of CHPAA or NPA was added to the nutrient solution, and the number of cluster roots was recorded after 5 days of inhibitor addition. To evaluate the effects of H<sup>+</sup> flux on cluster root formation, 30 µM Na<sub>3</sub>VO<sub>4</sub> was applied to plants for the duration of a 5-day treatment (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#Fig5">5c</a> and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#MOESM1">16c</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec30">DNA probe preparation for FISH</h3><p>A synthetic telomere oligo probe labeled with TexasRed-5-dUTP (provided by James A. Birchler, Univ. of Missouri, Columbia, MO, USA) was used for fluorescence in situ hybridization (FISH). The 170 bp repetitive sequence PCR amplified from lupin genomic DNA was labeled with Alexa Fluor 488-5-dUTP (Invitrogen Life Technologies) by the nick translation method<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 68" title="Kato, A., Lamb, J. C. &amp; Birchler, J. A. Chromosome painting using repetitive DNA sequences as probes for somatic chromosome identification in maize. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 101, 13554–13559 (2004)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#ref-CR68" id="ref-link-section-d56465e2632">68</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec31">Somatic chromosome preparation and FISH</h3><p>Metaphase chromosome isolation and chromosome spreading were performed according to Kato et al.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 68" title="Kato, A., Lamb, J. C. &amp; Birchler, J. A. Chromosome painting using repetitive DNA sequences as probes for somatic chromosome identification in maize. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 101, 13554–13559 (2004)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#ref-CR68" id="ref-link-section-d56465e2644">68</a></sup>. After preparation of slides with fluorescence-labeled probes and DAPI (4′,6-diamidino-2-phenylindole; Vector Laboratories, Burlingame, CA, USA), FISH images were captured by a Leica DFC340 FX Digital charge-coupled device camera under a fluorescence microscope (Leica Microsystems, Wetzlar, Germany). FISH images obtained from different channels were merged by Lecia CW4000 FISH software and analyzed with Lecia CW4000 Karyo software (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#MOESM1">1</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec32">Photosynthetic parameters monitoring</h3><p>After 4 weeks of P deficiency, the fifth fully expended leaf counting from the top of the plant was chosen for the measurements of net photosynthetic rate, stomatal conductance, and intercellular CO<sub>2</sub> concentration. Photosynthetic parameters were monitored by a portable photosynthesis system (LI-COR 6400-XT, LI-COR, Nebraska, USA) with a 2 × 3 cm<sup>2</sup> cuvette. The cuvette conditions were as follows: light intensity, 1000 μmol m<sup>−2</sup> s<sup>−1</sup>; flow rate, 500 μmol s<sup>−1</sup>; sample CO<sub>2</sub>, 400 μmol mol<sup>−1</sup>; and leaf temperature, 29 ± 2 °C (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#MOESM1">13a–c</a>). Pigments in leaf tissue were analyzed by spectrophotometry (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#MOESM1">14</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec33">Root exudate collection and analysis</h3><p>To collect root exudates, +P roots were incubated in a matrix solution containing 0.5 mM K<sub>2</sub>SO<sub>4</sub>, 0.5 mM Na<sub>2</sub>SO<sub>4</sub>, and 0.5 mM CaSO<sub>4</sub> (pH 6) for 1.5 h, while normal and cluster roots in the −P condition were separately collected. Organic acids in the exudates were determined by HPLC (Agilent 1200, Agilent Technologies, Germany) with an Agilent C18 AQ column (250 mm × 4.6 mm, 5 μm) and a flow rate of 0.5 mL min<sup>−1</sup>. The amount of organic acid was calculated from the peak area (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#MOESM1">15</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec34">Western blotting</h3><p>Total membrane proteins (15 µg) from roots were separated on 10% (w/v) sodium dodecyl sulfate (SDS)–polyacrylamide gel electrophoresis followed by electrotransfer onto a polyvinylidene difluoride membrane (0.45 µm, Merck Millipore Ltd, USA) with a semi-dry blotting system. H<sup>+</sup>-ATPase was detected using an anti-PMA2 polyclonal antibody (1:2000, v/v). Immunodetection was performed using a monoclonal anti-actin (plant) secondary antibody (Sigma). Intensities of H<sup>+</sup>-ATPase immunoreactive bands were quantified by Gel-Pro Analyzer software 4.0 (Media Cybernetics Inc., Bethesda, MD, USA) and expressed in terms of absolute integrated optical density (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#MOESM1">16a</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec35">Plasma membrane isolation and H<sup>+</sup>-ATPase activity assay</h3><p>Plasma membrane was isolated from three types of lupin roots: +P roots, −P normal roots, and −P cluster roots. The plasma membrane was isolated by using the method of aqueous two-phase partitioning<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Yan, F., Zhu, Y., Muller, C., Zorb, C. &amp; Schubert, S. Adaptation of H+-pumping and plasma membrane H+ ATPase activity in proteoid roots of white lupin under phosphate deficiency. Plant Physiol. 129, 50–63 (2002)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d56465e2731">39</a></sup>. Plasma membrane H<sup>+</sup>-ATPase activity was determined in a 0.5 mL reaction mixture containing 15 µg membrane, 50 mM BTP, 15 mM MgSO<sub>4</sub>, 7 mM KCl, and 0.25 mM Brij58 with or without 7 mM vanadate. The reaction was initiated by adding 10 mM ATP and terminated by 2.5 mL stop solution containing 2% (v/v) concentrated H<sub>2</sub>SO<sub>4</sub>, 5% (w/v) SDS, and 0.7% (w/v) (NH<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>MoO<sub>4</sub>. The absorbance at 700 nm was recorded after 30 min of incubation at room temperature. ATPase activity was calculated as P liberated in excess of vanadate addition control (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#MOESM1">16b</a>). The protein concentration in membrane preparations was determined by the Bradford reagent.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec36">Detection of H<sup>+</sup> flux by scanning ion-selective electrode technique</h3><p>The scanning ion-selective electrode technique was used to detect root H<sup>+</sup> flux. Cluster roots and 2–3 cm apical segments of normal roots were prepared immediately before detection. Roots were washed and immediately immersed and immobilized in the solution (0.1 mM KCl, 0.1 mM CaCl<sub>2</sub>, 0.1 mM MgCl<sub>2</sub>, 0.5 mM NaCl, 0.3 mM MES, 0.2 mM Na<sub>2</sub>SO<sub>4</sub>, pH 6.0) and allowed to equilibrate for 30 min. Then, the roots were moved to the chamber for analysis. H<sup>+</sup> flux was measured by moving an ion-selective microelectrode along the root at intervals of 30 μm, and the measured point was finally positioned to the elongation zone of the root (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#MOESM1">16b, c</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec37">AP activity assay</h3><p>AP activity was measured according to the manufacturer’s instructions (Nanjing Jiancheng Bioengineering Institute, China). One unit of AP activity was defined as 1 mg phenol liberation per min at 37 °C. Protein concentration was determined by the Bradford reagent (Supplementary Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#MOESM1">18</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#MOESM1">19</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec38">Construction for transformation vector</h3><p>The vector pFGC5941, consisting of the CAMV 35S promoter and kanamycin resistance gene, was used. The amplicon of the desired gene (<i>PAP10</i> and <i>PAP12</i>) from lupin complementary DNA (cDNA) was digested with two restriction enzymes <i>Bam</i>HI and <i>Sma</i>I, and then the gene was introduced into the T-DNA region of pFGC5941. The constructs were transformed into <i>Agrobacterium rhizogenes</i> strain K599 (Shanghai Weidi Biotechnology, China) using the heat-shock method according to the manufacturer’s instructions.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec39">White lupin transformation and gene function analysis</h3><p>Lupin seeds were surface sterilized by chlorine gas. The seeds were germinated on half strength Murashige and Skoog medium (PhytoTech Labs, USA) at 25 °C in darkness. After 2 days of germination, the hypocotyl and half of the cotyledon were removed, and the remaining cotyledon was soaked in <i>A. rhizogenes</i> solution for 20 min. The cotyledons were then placed on sterile filter paper and incubated at 22 °C in darkness for 3 days. After washing with a 300 mg L<sup>−1</sup> Timentin solution (Solarbio Life Sciences, China), cotyledons were transferred onto half strength MS solid medium containing 300 mg L<sup>−1</sup> Timentin (Plant Cell Technology, USA) for hairy root development. The <i>Bar</i> gene was PCR amplified to identify the transgenic roots, and the primers are shown in Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#MOESM1">15</a>. After 30 days of root development in darkness, hairy roots were transferred into P-omitted MS solution with or without 0.25 mM phytate (myo-inositolhexakisphosphate, sodium salt hydrate; Solarbio Life Sciences, China), and the roots were then sampled after 3 days of treatment.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec40">Isolation of RNA and quantitative real-time PCR</h3><p>Total RNA was extracted by TRIzol reagent (Invitrogen). The first-strand cDNAs were synthesized by a PrimeScript<sup>TM</sup> RT Reagent Kit (TaKaRa, DALIAN). Gene expression level was analyzed on a LightCycler Real-Time PCR Detection System (Bio-Rad) by using SYBR<sup>®</sup><i>Premix Ex Taq</i><sup>TM</sup> (TaKaRa, DALIAN). The primers used for quantitative real-time PCR (qRT-PCR) are shown in Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#MOESM1">15</a>. All quantifications were normalized to <i>ACTIN</i>. The qRT-PCR reactions were performed in triplicate for each of three independent samples. The relative expression of genes was calculated by the 2<sup>−∆∆Ct</sup> method<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 69" title="Livak, K. J. &amp; Schmittgen, T. D. Analysis of relative gene expression data using real-time quantitative PCR and the 2(−Delta Delta C(T)) Method. Methods 25, 402–408 (2001)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#ref-CR69" id="ref-link-section-d56465e2863">69</a></sup> (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#MOESM1">19a, b</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec41">Determination of P concentration</h3><p>For the determination of P concentration, plant tissues were dried and then grinded, and the resulting sample was digested in concentrated H<sub>2</sub>SO<sub>4</sub>. P concentration was spectrophotometrically determined by the ammonium molybdate/vanadate method (Supplementary Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#MOESM1">10</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#MOESM1">20</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec42">Reporting summary</h3><p>Further information on research design is available in the <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#MOESM3">Nature Research Reporting Summary</a> linked to this article.</p></div></div></section>
                

                <section aria-labelledby="data-availability" data-title="Data availability"><div class="c-article-section" id="data-availability-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="data-availability">Data availability</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="data-availability-content">
              
              <p>Data supporting the findings of this work are available within the paper and its Supplementary Information files. A reporting summary for this article is available as a Supplementary Information file. The datasets generated and analyzed in the current study are available from the corresponding author on reasonable request. All the genome and annotation datasets of white lupin were deposited to the NCBI BioProject database under accession <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/bioproject/PRJNA592024">PRJNA592024</a>. The assembled genome sequences of white lupin have been deposited at DDBJ/ENA/GenBank under the accession JAAEJY000000000. The version described in this paper is version <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nuccore/JAAEJY000000000">JAAEJY010000000</a>. The white lupin genome datasets are also freely available through <a href="http://brassicadb.org/brad/pub/genomes/Lalbus/">http://brassicadb.org/brad/pub/genomes/Lalbus/</a>. The two public mRNA-seq datasets in white lupin under P deficiency were downloaded from the NCBI database under accession <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sra/?term=SRA145661">SRA145661</a> and <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/geo/query/acc.cgi?acc=GSE31132">GSE31132</a>. Genome datasets of <i>A. thaliana</i> were downloaded from the TAIR database (TAIR10; <a href="http://www.arabidopsis.org/index.jsp">http://www.arabidopsis.org/index.jsp</a>). Genome sequences of other Legume species were downloaded from the legume information system (<a href="https://legumeinfo.org/">https://legumeinfo.org/</a>) as well as the Phytozome database (<a href="https://phytozome.jgi.doe.gov/">https://phytozome.jgi.doe.gov/</a>). The source data underlying Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#Fig1">1b</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#Fig3">3</a>, and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#Fig5">5a–c</a>, as well as Supplementary Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#MOESM6">10</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#MOESM6">13</a>–<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#MOESM6">16</a>, and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#MOESM6">18</a>–<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14891-z#MOESM6">20</a> are provided as a Source Data file.</p>

    <a data-track="click" data-track-action="outbound reference" data-track-label="link" href="/articles/cas-redirect/1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2sXivVOnsro%253D" aria-label="View reference 6 on CAS">CAS</a>

    <a data-track="click" data-track-action="outbound reference" data-track-label="link" aria-label="Search for reference 6 on Google Scholar" href="http://scholar.google.com/scholar_lookup?&amp;title=A%20comprehensive%20draft%20genome%20sequence%20for%20lupin%20%28Lupinus%20angustifolius%29%2C%20an%20emerging%20health%20food%3A%20insights%20into%20plant-microbe%20interactions%20and%20legume%20evolution&amp;journal=Plant%20Biotechnol.%20J.&amp;volume=15&amp;pages=318-330&amp;publication_year=2017&amp;author=Hane%2CJK">
